Question: Is the Plan completed?
Provide a short reasoning before deciding.

[trial_qa_imaginator] The plan has **not** been completed.

**Reasoning:**  
- The intended action is to perform a `look` (or `inspect`) to scan for a potato.  
- You are currently standing on a **closed, unlocked door** at (4, 4). In this state the environment blocks any `look` operation (“you must open the door first before you can look through it”).  
- Since the required `look` action cannot be executed while the door is closed, the plan remains unfinished.  

You need to open the door first (e.g., `open door_6`) and then issue the `look` command.

Question: Is the plan still promising? Answer no when recent actions produced no new state change, no progress toward the focused plan step, or when the agent is repeating actions that do not modify the situation.

[trial_imaginator] **Naïve “search‑and‑place” plan**

The room is small (0 ≤ x ≤ 4, 0 ≤ y ≤ 4) and we already know the positions of the only fixed objects (the open door at (4, 4) and the empty table at (0, 3)).  
The simplest way to guarantee that we will eventually see the potato is to walk to a few representative points that together give us a complete view of the whole room, looking after each move.  

When the potato appears in the field of view we can **take** it, walk to the table, and **place** it there.

Below is the minimal sequence of human‑executable actions that follows this naïve strategy.

---

### Step‑by‑step action list

| # | Action | Reason |
|---|--------|--------|
| 1 | `look` | Verify what we can see from the current spot (2, 2). |
| 2 | `move 0 0` | Go to the bottom‑left corner – a new viewing angle. |
| 3 | `look` | Check for the potato. |
| 4 | `move 0 4` | Go to the top‑left corner – another new angle. |
| 5 | `look` | Check again. |
| 6 | `move 4 0` | Go to the bottom‑right corner – cover the opposite side. |
| 7 | `look` | Check again. |
| 8 | `move 4 4` | Go to the top‑right corner (the door location) – final angle. |
| 9 | `look` | If the potato still isn’t visible, it must be hidden behind something; we have now surveyed every corner. |
|10| **If the potato was seen in any of the previous looks**: `take potato` | Pick it up. |
|11| `move 0 3` | Walk to the table’s coordinates. |
|12| `place potato` | Put the potato on the table. |

---

### How the plan works

1. **Systematic coverage** – By visiting the four corners (0,0), (0,4), (4,0) and (4,4) we obtain a full 360° sweep of the room. Because the door is already open, there are no visual obstructions.
2. **“Look” after each move** – The `look` command reports every object currently in sight. As soon as the potato becomes visible we stop moving and proceed to the next phase.
3. **Take‑and‑place** – Once the potato is in the inventory we simply walk to the known table location (0, 3) and use `place` to satisfy the goal.

If the potato is unusually hidden (e.g., behind a wall that we cannot see from any corner), the above four‑corner scan will still be the simplest “naïve” approach; we could then add a few extra interior points (e.g., (2,0), (2,4), (0,2), (4,2)) and repeat the `look`/`move` pattern. But for most standard rooms the corner sweep is sufficient.

---

**Resulting minimal command sequence (assuming the potato shows up at the first corner where it is visible):**

```
move 0 0
look
move 0 4
look
move 4 0
look
move 4 4
look
take potato
move 0 3
place potato
```

- [352] Place a potato on the table. (current focus)

[decompose_qa_imaginator] **Reasoning**

The high‑level goal *“place a potato on the table”* requires the following sub‑steps:

1. **Find the potato** – it is not currently visible from any of the positions we have examined.
2. **Take the potato** – once it is located we must pick it up.
3. **Return to the table** (at (0, 3)) – if we are not already there.

[trial_imaginator] **Naïve “grid‑search” strategy**

1. **Walk the whole floor, looking at every square.**  
   The room “main” is bounded by x ≤ 4 and y ≤ 4, so the simplest way to be sure you’ll see the potato is to visit each integer coordinate (0 … 4, 0 … 4) and run a `look` after every move.

2. **When the potato appears, pick it up.**  
   As soon as a `look` reports a visible object called `potato`, execute `take potato`.

3. **Carry the potato to the table and put it down.**  
   The table is at (0, 3).  From wherever you are, move to (0, 3) and run `place potato`.

### Concrete list of simple actions (you can copy‑paste them one after another)

```
# ----- systematic scan -----
move 0 0   ; look
move 0 1   ; look
move 0 2   ; look
move 0 3   ; look   # table is here (empty)
move 0 4   ; look
move 1 4   ; look
move 1 3   ; look

move 1 0   ; look

move 3 0   ; look

# ----- as soon as a "potato" shows up in the output -----
take potato

# ----- go to the table and set it down -----
move 0 3   ; place potato
```

**Explanation of why this works**

* The `move X Y` commands keep you inside the known room limits (0 ≤ X ≤ 4, 0 ≤ Y ≤ 4).  
* After each move we run `look`; `look` reports **all** objects that are currently visible from that square.  
* Because we visit every square in the room, the potato cannot hide in a location you haven’t inspected.  
* Once the potato is in your inventory, moving to the table’s coordinate (0, 3) and `place`‑ing it satisfies the original goal (“place a potato on the table”).

You can stop the scan early as soon as the `look` command tells you the potato is visible; the remaining moves are then unnecessary. The above list is the most straightforward, “human‑readable” way to guarantee you’ll find the potato and put it on the table.
